<p><b>Martin Van Buren</b> (<a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl"><i>Maarten van Buren</i></span><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup><span class="unicode haudio" style="white-space:nowrap;"><span class="fn"><a href="/wiki/File:Maarten_van_Buren.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;<a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Maarten_van_Buren.ogg" class="internal" title="Maarten van Buren.ogg">pronunciation</a></span>&#160;<small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Maarten_van_Buren.ogg" title="File:Maarten van Buren.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>; December 5, 1782 – July 24, 1862) was an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> politician who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">eighth</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (1837–1841). A member of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>, he served in a number of senior roles, including <a href="/wiki/List_of_Vice_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Vice Presidents of the United States">eighth</a> <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> (1833–1837) and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> (1829–1831), both under <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. Van Buren's inability as president to deal with the economic chaos of the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a> and with the surging <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whig Party</a> led to his defeat in the 1840 election.</p>
<p>Of Dutch descent, Van Buren learned early how to coordinate multiple ethnic and political groups. A meticulous dresser, he could mingle in upper class society as well as in saloon environments such as the tavern his father ran.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> A delegate to a political convention at age 18, he quickly moved from local to state politics, gaining fame both as a political organizer and an accomplished lawyer. Elected to the Senate by the state legislature in 1821, Van Buren supported <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a> for president in 1824, but by 1828 had come to support General Andrew Jackson. Van Buren was a major supporter and organizer for Jackson in the 1828 election. Jackson was elected, and made Van Buren <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>.</p>
<p>During Jackson's eight years as president, Van Buren was a key advisor, and built the organizational structure for the coalescing Democratic Party, particularly in New York. In 1831, Jackson gave him a <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">recess appointment</a> as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">American minister to Britain</a>, but Van Buren's nomination was rejected by the Senate, cutting short his service in London. He was successful in the jockeying to become Jackson's picked successor, and was elected vice president in 1832. Van Buren faced several Whig opponents in his presidential bid in 1836, and was elected.</p>
<p>As president, Van Buren was blamed for the depression of 1837; hostile newspapers called him "Martin Van Ruin". He attempted to cure the economic problems by keeping control of federal funds in an <a href="/wiki/Independent_Treasury" title="Independent Treasury">independent treasury</a>—rather than in state banks—but Congress would not approve of this until 1840. In foreign affairs, he wanted the United States not to annex <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> (the 10th president <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> would annex the state eight years after Van Buren's initial rejection). Additionally, relations with Britain and its colonies in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> proved to be strained from the bloodless <a href="/wiki/Aroostook_War" title="Aroostook War">Aroostook War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caroline_affair" title="Caroline affair"><i>Caroline</i> Affair</a>.</p>
<p>In 1840, Van Buren was voted out of office, losing to Whig candidate <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a>. Van Buren was the leading candidate for the <a href="/wiki/1844_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1844 Democratic National Convention">Democratic nomination in 1844</a>, but lost to <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>, who <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1844" title="United States presidential election, 1844">went on to win the election</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">1848 election</a> Van Buren ran unsuccessfully as the candidate of the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a>. He also supported fellow Democrats <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> (1852), <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> (1856), and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_A._Douglas" title="Stephen A. Douglas">Stephen A. Douglas</a> (1860) for the presidency, but his increasingly <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">abolitionist</a> views and support for the <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> led him to support <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>'s policies after the start of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.</p>
<p>Van Buren's health began to fail in 1861, and he died in July 1862 at the age of seventy-nine. Although he served in many high offices, his most lasting achievement was as a political organizer who built the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)" class="mw-redirect">modern Democratic Party</a> and guided it to dominance in the new <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MVBBirth.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="A bronze marker with a map of the State of New York at the top, under which is the word Birth site and other text" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/MVBBirth.jpeg/220px-MVBBirth.jpeg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/MVBBirth.jpeg/330px-MVBBirth.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1f/MVBBirth.jpeg/440px-MVBBirth.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3472" data-file-height="2604" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MVBBirth.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Historical_marker" title="Historical marker">Historical marker</a> located at the birthplace of Martin Van Buren</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg/220px-Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg/330px-Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg/440px-Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3678" data-file-height="4432" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren_Baptism_record.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Baptismal record from Kinderhook Dutch Reformed Church. Van Buren's name was spelled "Maarten" in Dutch, and he was baptized on December 15, 1782</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Born on December 5, 1782, Martin Van Buren was the first president to be born after the United States <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">declared independence</a>. He was <a href="/wiki/Baptized" title="Baptized" class="mw-redirect">baptized</a> on December 15 of that year as "Maarten van Buren", the original <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> spelling of his name. He was born in the village of <a href="/wiki/Kinderhook_(village),_New_York" title="Kinderhook (village), New York">Kinderhook, New York</a>, on December 5, 1782, about 20 miles (32&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a>. In the era before the steamboat, Kinderhook was an isolated village, and most of the townsfolk, including the Van Burens, were of Dutch descent and spoke Dutch at home. Martin Van Buren was the only president who spoke <a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">English</a> as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_languages_spoken" title="List of Presidents of the United States by languages spoken" class="mw-redirect">a second language</a>. Van Buren descended from Cornelis Maessen of the town of <a href="/wiki/Buren" title="Buren">Buren</a> in the Netherlands, who had come to America in 1631 and purchased a plot of land on Manhattan Island; his son, Martin Cornelisen, took the surname Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The future president's father, Abraham Van Buren (1737–1817), was a farmer who owned a Kinderhook inn as well as six slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Abraham Van Buren supported the American Revolution as a captain in the <a href="/wiki/Van_Alstyne%27s_Regiment_of_Militia" title="Van Alstyne's Regiment of Militia">Albany County Militia's 7th Regiment</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> and later joined the Jeffersonian Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> He was active in local politics and government, and served as Kinderhook's town clerk from 1787 to 1797.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> Martin Van Buren's mother was Maria Hoes Van Alen Van Buren (1747–1818). She had been married to Johannes Van Alen. After Johannes' death, she married Abraham Van Buren in 1776. By his mother's first marriage, Van Buren had one half-sister and two half-brothers, including <a href="/wiki/James_I._Van_Alen" title="James I. Van Alen">James I. Van Alen</a>, who practiced law with Van Buren for a time and served as a member of Congress (1807–1809). Van Buren had four full siblings:<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Dirckie "Derike" Van Buren (1777–1865), the wife of Barent Hoes (1777-1853). Barent Hoes was the brother of Martin Van Buren's wife, and served in local office including town clerk.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Jannetje (Called "Hannah" or "Jane") Van Buren (1780-1838), who never married and resided with her sister Dirckie Van Buren and brother-in-law Barent Hoes.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Lawrence Van Buren (1786–1868), an attorney who served as a militia officer in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> and held local offices including <a href="/wiki/Town_Supervisor" title="Town Supervisor" class="mw-redirect">Town Supervisor</a> of Kinderhook, postmaster, and presidential elector <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1852" title="United States presidential election, 1852">in 1852</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>Abraham Van Buren (1788–1836), an attorney who served as Columbia County Surrogate after Martin Van Buren and James I. Van Alen.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Van Buren received a basic education at the village schoolhouse and briefly studied <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Kinderhook_Academy" title="Kinderhook Academy" class="mw-redirect">Kinderhook Academy</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Washington_Seminary" title="Washington Seminary" class="mw-redirect">Washington Seminary</a> in Claverack.<sup id="cite_ref-nycourts_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nycourts-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> His formal education ended before he reached 14, when he began <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">reading law</a> in 1796 at the office of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Silvester_(1734%E2%80%931808)" title="Peter Silvester (1734–1808)">Peter Silvester</a> and his son Francis, prominent <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a> attorneys in Kinderhook.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren was small in stature; as an adult he was 5 feet 6 inches tall, and often referred to as "Little Van."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> When he first began his legal studies, he often presented an unkempt appearance in rough, homespun clothing.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> It was the Silvesters who suggested that Van Buren could improve his professional prospects by dressing fashionably and taking care in how he appeared in public; he heeded the advice and patterned his clothing, appearance, bearing and conduct after theirs.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> After six years under the Silvesters, the elder Silvester and Democratic-Republican political figure <a href="/wiki/John_Peter_Van_Ness" title="John Peter Van Ness">John Peter Van Ness</a> suggested that Van Buren's political leanings made it a good idea for him to complete his education with a Democratic-Republican attorney. Accepting this advice, he spent a final year of apprenticeship in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> office of John Van Ness's brother <a href="/wiki/William_P._Van_Ness" title="William P. Van Ness">William P. Van Ness</a>, a political lieutenant of <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren was <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_practice_law" title="Admission to practice law">admitted to the bar</a> in 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren married <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Van_Buren" title="Hannah Van Buren">Hannah Hoes</a>, his childhood sweetheart and first cousin once removed, on February 21, 1807, in <a href="/wiki/Catskill,_New_York" title="Catskill, New York" class="mw-redirect">Catskill, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Like Van Buren, she was raised in a Dutch home; she spoke primarily Dutch, and spoke English with a distinct accent.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple had five sons and one daughter: <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Van_Buren" title="Abraham Van Buren">Abraham</a> (1807–1873) a graduate of West Point and career military officer; <a href="/wiki/John_Van_Buren" title="John Van Buren">John</a> (1810–1866), graduate of Yale and Attorney General of New York; Martin, Jr. (1812–1855), secretary to his father and editor of his father's papers until a premature death from tuberculosis; Winfield Scott (born and died in 1814); and Smith Thompson (1817–1876), an editor and special assistant to his father while president.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Their daughter was stillborn.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> After 12 years of marriage, Hannah Van Buren contracted <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> and died on February 5, 1819, at the age of 35.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Martin Van Buren never remarried.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Van Buren had been active in politics from at least the age of 17 when he attended a party convention in <a href="/wiki/Troy,_New_York" title="Troy, New York">Troy, New York</a>, where he worked successfully to secure for <a href="/wiki/John_Peter_Van_Ness" title="John Peter Van Ness">John Peter Van Ness</a> the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> nomination in a special election for the <a href="/wiki/New_York%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="New York's 6th congressional district">6th Congressional District</a> seat.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> He formed a law partnership with his half-brother <a href="/wiki/James_I._Van_Alen" title="James I. Van Alen">James I. Van Alen</a>, and once established in his practice, he became financially secure enough to increase his focus on politics.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>, and allied himself with the <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton faction</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>. Van Buren supported <a href="/wiki/Daniel_D._Tompkins" title="Daniel D. Tompkins">Daniel D. Tompkins</a> for Governor over incumbent <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Lewis_(governor)" title="Morgan Lewis (governor)">Morgan Lewis</a> in 1807. Tompkins won, and his allies were a majority in the state legislature. As a result, Van Buren was appointed <a href="/wiki/Probate_court" title="Probate court">Surrogate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_County,_New_York" title="Columbia County, New York">Columbia County, New York</a>, replacing Van Alen, who had supported Lewis. Van Buren served as Surrogate from 1808 until 1813, when the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> obtained a majority in the state legislature and replaced him.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren was a member of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Senate" title="New York State Senate">New York State Senate</a> from 1812 to 1820, and joined the <a href="/wiki/Bucktails" title="Bucktails">opposition party</a> in 1813. (The opposition party were Democratic-Republicans who fought <a href="/wiki/DeWitt_Clinton" title="DeWitt Clinton">DeWitt Clinton</a> for control of the Democratic-Republican Party in New York.) Van Buren served as <a href="/wiki/New_York_Attorney_General" title="New York Attorney General">New York Attorney General</a> from 1815 to 1819. He replaced <a href="/wiki/William_Floyd" title="William Floyd">William Floyd</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_elector" title="Presidential elector" class="mw-redirect">presidential elector</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1820" title="United States presidential election, 1820">1820</a>, and voted for <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_D._Tompkins" title="Daniel D. Tompkins">Daniel D. Tompkins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Though he never served in the military, during the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> Van Buren worked in the State Senate to pass war measures, including bills to expand the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Army_National_Guard" title="New York Army National Guard">New York Militia</a> and increase soldier pay. In addition, he was a special judge advocate appointed to serve as one of the prosecutors of <a href="/wiki/William_Hull" title="William Hull">William Hull</a> during Hull's court-martial following the surrender of <a href="/wiki/Detroit,_Michigan" title="Detroit, Michigan" class="mw-redirect">Detroit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At first he opposed DeWitt Clinton's plan for the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a>, but he supported it when the <a href="/wiki/Bucktails" title="Bucktails">Bucktails</a> (the name given to the anti-DeWitt Clinton Democratic-Republicans) were able to gain a majority on the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal_Commission" title="Erie Canal Commission">Erie Canal Commission</a>, and he supported a bill that raised money for the canal through the sale of state bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1817, Van Buren's connection with so-called "machine politics" started when he created the first political organization encompassing all of New York, the Bucktails.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The Bucktails became a successful movement that emphasized party loyalty and used it to capture and control many patronage posts throughout New York. Van Buren gained the nickname of "Little Magician" for the skill with which he exploited what came to be called the "<a href="/wiki/Spoils_system" title="Spoils system">spoils system</a>". Van Buren served as a member of the 1820 state <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_meeting)" title="Constitutional convention (political meeting)">constitutional convention</a>, where he favored expanded voting rights, but opposed universal suffrage and tried to maintain property requirements for voting.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He was the leading figure in the <a href="/wiki/Albany_Regency" title="Albany Regency">Albany Regency</a>, a group of Bucktail leaders who for more than a generation dominated the politics of New York and influenced national politics. The Regency, together with other political organizations such as <a href="/wiki/Tammany_Hall" title="Tammany Hall">Tammany Hall</a>, played a major role in expanding the spoils system and making it a recognized and accepted procedure. He was the prime architect of the first nationwide political party: the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_Democrats" title="Jacksonian Democrats" class="mw-redirect">Jacksonian Democrats</a> or <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a>, which evolved from the Democratic-Republicans and relied on party loyalty and patronage to prevent contentious sectional issues, including tariffs and slavery, from becoming national crises. In Van Buren's words, "Without strong national political organizations, there would be nothing to moderate the prejudices between free and slaveholding states."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> As had <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> and other Democratic Party organizers who favored <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States' rights">states' rights</a> and local control, Van Buren was struggling to find an institutional solution to the Constitution's seeming inability to prevent concentration of power in an <a href="/wiki/Administrative_republic" title="Administrative republic" class="mw-redirect">administrative republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999102_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDerthick1999102-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Early in his life Van Buren owned a slave, a man named Tom who served as his personal valet. Tom ran away in 1814 and eventually settled in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, with Van Buren making no effort to locate him. In 1824 Tom was found to be living in <a href="/wiki/Worcester,_Massachusetts" title="Worcester, Massachusetts">Worcester, Massachusetts</a>. Since he still legally owned Tom (under New York's gradual emancipation law, slavery was scheduled to be completely abolished in the state in 1827), Van Buren agreed to sell Tom to the finder, a resident of <a href="/wiki/Rensselaer_County,_New_York" title="Rensselaer County, New York">Rensselaer County</a>, for $50 provided that he could be captured without violence. The finder could not make such a guarantee, and his willingness to pay was lessened by the knowledge that Tom would be emancipated in less than three years even if he was re-enslaved, so Tom remained free, as Van Buren probably intended.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Senate_and_national_politics">U.S. Senate and national politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: U.S. Senate and national politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MVanBuren.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/MVanBuren.png/300px-MVanBuren.png" width="300" height="378" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/MVanBuren.png/450px-MVanBuren.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/MVanBuren.png/600px-MVanBuren.png 2x" data-file-width="2405" data-file-height="3029" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MVanBuren.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gubernatorial <a href="/wiki/Portrait" title="Portrait">portrait</a> of Martin Van Buren.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1821" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1821">February 1821</a>, Martin Van Buren was elected a <a href="/wiki/U.S._Senator_from_New_York" title="U.S. Senator from New York" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Senator from New York</a>, defeating the incumbent <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Sanford" title="Nathan Sanford">Nathan Sanford</a> who ran as the <a href="/wiki/DeWitt_Clinton" title="DeWitt Clinton">Clintonian</a> candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren at first favored <a href="/wiki/Internal_improvements" title="Internal improvements">internal improvements</a>, such as road repairs and canal construction, proposing a constitutional amendment in 1824 to authorize such undertakings, but changed his position the following year. He voted for the <a href="/wiki/Tariff_in_American_history" title="Tariff in American history" class="mw-redirect">tariffs</a> of 1824 and 1828, and then gradually abandoned this protectionist position, later coming out for tariffs "for revenue only."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824" title="United States presidential election, 1824">presidential election of 1824</a>, Van Buren supported <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a> and received the electoral vote of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> for <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Vice President of the United States of America" class="mw-redirect">Vice President</a>. None of the presidential candidates—Crawford, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>—had received a majority of the electoral college votes, so <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1824#1825_Contingent_election" title="United States presidential election, 1824">the choice fell</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a>. The House had to choose from among the top three candidates, so Clay was eliminated. Van Buren had originally hoped to block <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> by denying him the state of New York, which was divided between supporters of Crawford and Adams. However, Representative <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Van_Rensselaer" title="Stephen Van Rensselaer">Stephen Van Rensselaer</a> swung New York to Adams. Adams won, and appointed Clay as Secretary of State. Because Clay had supported Adams in the House election, Jackson and Crawford supporters alleged corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> After the House contest, Van Buren shrewdly kept out of <a href="/wiki/Corrupt_Bargain#Election_of_1824" title="Corrupt Bargain">the controversy which followed</a>, and began looking forward to 1828. He switched his support early from Crawford, whose ill health after a stroke had made him a less than viable candidate, to <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, who had won the popular vote in 1824. Jackson was angered to see the presidency go to Adams after he received fewer votes than Jackson, and Jackson eagerly looked forward to a rematch.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Always notably courteous in his treatment of opponents, Van Buren showed no bitterness toward either Adams or <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a>, and he voted for Clay's confirmation as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, notwithstanding Jackson's "<a href="/wiki/Corrupt_bargain" title="Corrupt bargain" class="mw-redirect">corrupt bargain</a>" charge.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time, he opposed the Adams-Clay plans for internal infrastructure improvements (roads, canals, bridges etc.) and declined to support U.S. participation in the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Panama" title="Congress of Panama">Congress of Panama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> As chair of the Judiciary Committee, he brought forward a number of measures for the improvement of judicial procedure, including one (not adopted), which would have required a super-majority vote by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">United States Supreme Court</a> to declare a law unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> in May 1826, Van Buren joined with Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)" class="mw-redirect">Thomas Hart Benton</a> in reporting on patronage in the executive branch, going against his own use of the spoils system to propose unsuccessfully that Presidents not be able to remove officeholders at will, and that Presidents report to Congress on the reasons why dismissed holders of federal positions had been removed.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The 1828 "<a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1828" title="Tariff of 1828" class="mw-redirect">Tariff of Abominations</a>" was recognized as his work. Since Democrats, especially Southerners, were generally opposed to tariffs that increased the price of manufactured goods from the North but did not benefit the raw materials produced in the South, Van Buren could normally have been expected to oppose tariffs. Political observers of the time viewed Van Buren's efforts to pass the 1828 tariff as part of the campaign to elect Jackson as President. Anticipating that most Southerners would vote for Andrew Jackson no matter who else was running, Van Buren intended the tariff proposed by Jackson's Northern Democratic supporters in Congress to attract to Jackson's candidacy Northern voters, who generally favored high tariffs to protect the manufactured goods they produced. Van Buren voted in favor, later adopting the cover story that he had done so only in response to instructions from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_State_Legislature" title="New York State Legislature">New York State Legislature</a>. Most Democrats, especially Southerners, continued to oppose tariffs after 1828. Van Buren's political opponents in the Democratic Party used his 1828 vote against him for years afterwards to prevent him from obtaining Southern support for his candidacies.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren was not an orator, but his more important speeches show careful preparation and his opinions carried weight; the oft-repeated charge that he refrained from declaring himself on crucial questions is hardly borne out by an examination of his senatorial career. In <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_election_in_New_York,_1827" title="United States Senate election in New York, 1827">February 1827</a>, he was re-elected to the Senate by a large majority. He became one of the recognized managers of the Jackson campaign, and his tour of <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Carolinas" title="Carolinas" class="mw-redirect">Carolinas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> in the spring of 1827 won support for Jackson from Crawford. Martin Van Buren sought to reorganize and unify "the old Republican party" behind Jackson.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> At the state level, Jackson's committee chairs would split up the responsibilities around the state and organize volunteers at the local level. "Hurra Boys" would plant hickory trees (in honor of Jackson's nickname, "Old Hickory") or hand out hickory sticks at rallies. In 1828 Van Buren ran for <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_New_York" title="Governor of New York">Governor of New York</a> in an effort to use his personal popularity to bolster Jackson's chances of carrying New York in the presidential election. Jackson defeated Adams handily, leading the pro-Adams <i>New York American</i> to editorialize "Organization is the secret of victory. By the want of it we have been overthrown."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren won his election, and resigned from the Senate to start the gubernatorial term, which began on January 1, 1829.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Martin Van Buren's tenure as New York governor is the second shortest on record. While his term was short, he did manage to pass the Bank Safety Fund Law (an early form of deposit insurance) through the Legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Jackson_Cabinet">Jackson Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Jackson Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On March 5, 1829, President Jackson appointed Van Buren <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, an office which probably had been assured to him before the 1828 elections, and Van Buren resigned the governorship on March 12.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> He was succeeded in the governorship by his Lieutenant Governor, <a href="/wiki/Enos_T._Throop" title="Enos T. Throop">Enos T. Throop</a>, a member of the Regency. As Secretary of State, Van Buren took care to keep on good terms with the <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_Cabinet" title="Kitchen Cabinet">Kitchen Cabinet</a>, the group of politicians who acted as Jackson's advisers. He sometimes opposed Jackson in the matter of removing political appointees from office to replace them with Jackson loyalists, but also saw to the replacement of Postmasters in New York with Van Buren loyalists.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He won the lasting regard of Jackson by his courtesies to <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Eaton" title="Peggy Eaton" class="mw-redirect">Peggy Eaton</a>, wife of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/John_H._Eaton" title="John H. Eaton" class="mw-redirect">John H. Eaton</a>, with whom the wives of the cabinet officers led by Vice President Calhoun's wife, <a href="/wiki/Floride_Calhoun" title="Floride Calhoun">Floride Calhoun</a> had refused to associate in the <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_Affair" title="Petticoat Affair" class="mw-redirect">Petticoat Affair</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_rejected_Minister.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Cartoon image of an older man riding on the back of another older man and stumbling toward the steps of a building labeled &quot;Capitol&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_rejected_Minister.jpg/220px-The_rejected_Minister.jpg" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_rejected_Minister.jpg/330px-The_rejected_Minister.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_rejected_Minister.jpg/440px-The_rejected_Minister.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1656" data-file-height="1943" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_rejected_Minister.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1832 Whig cartoon shows <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Jackson</a> carrying Van Buren into office</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>No serious diplomatic questions arose during Van Buren's tenure, but he achieved several notable successes, including the settlement of long-standing claims against France, winning reparations for property that had been seized during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>. He reached an agreement with the British to open trade with the <a href="/wiki/British_West_Indies" title="British West Indies">British West Indies</a> colonies. In addition, Van Buren completed a treaty with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> that gained American merchants access to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Items on which he did not achieve success included settling the <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>-<a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> boundary dispute with Great Britain, gaining settlement of the U.S. claim to the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>, concluding a commercial treaty with <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, and persuading <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> to sell <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martin_Van_Buren_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Van_Buren-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren also advised Jackson informally on matters of domestic policy. In the controversy over the <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">Bank of the United States</a>, he sided with Jackson. He also sided with Jackson on the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin_Van_Buren_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Van_Buren-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> After the breach between Jackson and Calhoun, which culminated with the <a href="/wiki/Nullification_Crisis" title="Nullification Crisis">Nullification Crisis</a>, Van Buren's position as one of Jackson's primary political supporters and policy advisors clearly marked him as the most prominent candidate for the vice presidency in 1832, and Jackson's most likely successor in 1836.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vice-Presidency">Vice-Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vice-Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 1829, Jackson had already made known his wish that Van Buren receive the 1832 vice presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> In April 1831, Van Buren resigned as Secretary of State during the <a href="/wiki/Petticoat_affair" title="Petticoat affair">Petticoat affair</a>, giving Jackson the opportunity to end the dispute by requesting other resignations so he could reorganize his cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren did not leave office until June, and continued to play a part in the <a href="/wiki/Kitchen_Cabinet" title="Kitchen Cabinet">Kitchen Cabinet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> In August 1831 Jackson gave Van Buren a <a href="/wiki/Recess_appointment" title="Recess appointment">recess appointment</a> as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">Minister to the Court of St. James (Britain)</a> and he arrived in London in September.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> He was cordially received, but in February, he learned that his nomination had been rejected by the Senate on January 25, 1832.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> The rejection was attributed by the Senate to Van Buren's instructions while Secretary of State to <a href="/wiki/Louis_McLane" title="Louis McLane">Louis McLane</a>, the American minister to Britain. Van Buren's instructions, which concerned the opening of the West Indies trade, supposedly repudiated the foreign policy of Jackson's predecessors, which the Senate claimed was a breach of decorum.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, the rejection of Van Buren was the work of <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">Calhoun</a>. Calhoun opposed Van Buren, believing that Van Buren had attempted to keep him from becoming vice president. Calhoun also opposed Van Buren for his role in the Petticoat Affair and his work on the 1828 tariff. When the vote on Van Buren's nomination was taken, enough pro-Calhoun Democrats refrained from voting to produce a tie, thus giving Calhoun, in his role as presiding officer, the ability to cast a vote. He voted no, and so achieved "vengeance" on Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Calhoun was elated, convinced that he had ended Van Buren's career. "It will kill him dead, sir, kill him dead. He will never kick, sir, never kick," Calhoun exclaimed to a friend within earshot of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hart_Benton_(senator)" title="Thomas Hart Benton (senator)" class="mw-redirect">Thomas Hart Benton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> In fact, Calhoun's move backfired by making Van Buren seem the victim of petty politics, thus raising him in both Jackson's regard and the esteem of others in the Democratic Party. Far from ending Van Buren's career, Calhoun's action gave greater impetus to Van Buren's candidacy for vice president.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After a brief tour of Europe, Van Buren reached New York on July 5, 1832. The May <a href="/wiki/1832_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1832 Democratic National Convention">1832 Democratic National Convention</a>, the party's first, had nominated him for vice president on the Jackson ticket.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren's nomination was not as strongly supported as Jackson's, particularly among southerners who recalled his work on the tariff in 1828, but he somewhat placated southerners by denying the right of Congress to abolish slavery in the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a> without the consent of the slave states.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Jackson-Van Buren ticket won the 1832 election, and Van Buren took office as Vice President in March 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> During his time in office Van Buren continued to be one of Jackson's primary advisors and confidants, and accompanied Jackson on his tour of the northeastern United States in 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson's confidence in Van Buren was further demonstrated after Jackson named <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Butler_(lawyer)" title="Benjamin Franklin Butler (lawyer)">Benjamin F. Butler</a>, Van Buren's political ally and former law partner, to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Forsyth_(Georgia)" title="John Forsyth (Georgia)">John Forsyth</a>, another Van Buren ally, to serve as Secretary of State.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren's support of Jackson in the Nullification Crisis and the decision not to recharter the Second Bank of the United States made him a target of Jackson's most vocal opponents. Van Buren was threatened with violence, including explicit threats from Senator <a href="/wiki/George_Poindexter" title="George Poindexter">George Poindexter</a> of Mississippi, which caused Van Buren to carry pistols for self-defense. However he also demonstrated both the willingness and the ability to work with his opponents, cooperating with Clay and Calhoun (now a Senator) to pass the compromise <a href="/wiki/Tariff_of_1833" title="Tariff of 1833">Tariff of 1833</a>, which helped end the Nullification Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During one contentious debate on the bank issue, Van Buren presided over the Senate as Clay spoke passionately about the harm he believed Jackson's bank policy would cause. Directing his remarks to Van Buren, Clay asked rhetorically whether Van Buren would approach Jackson and persuade him to change his mind. After Clay concluded, observers wondered how Van Buren would respond. Rather than answering directly, Van Buren descended from the rostrum and asked Clay if he could borrow a pinch of Clay's <a href="/wiki/Snuff_(tobacco)" title="Snuff (tobacco)">snuff</a>. Caught off guard, Clay reflexively handed over his <a href="/wiki/Snuff_box" title="Snuff box" class="mw-redirect">snuff box</a>. Van Buren took a pinch, bowed to Clay, and left the chamber, both reducing the effect of Clay's remarks and preventing tension from escalating, as would have happened if Van Buren had attempted to reply to Clay.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1836">Election of 1836</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Election of 1836">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1836" title="United States presidential election, 1836">United States presidential election, 1836</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1832" title="U.S. presidential election, 1832" class="mw-redirect">election of 1832</a>, the Jackson-Van Buren ticket won by a landslide.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackson, not running in 1836, was determined to make Van Buren his successor in order to continue the Jackson administration's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren was <a href="/wiki/Unanimity" title="Unanimity">unanimously</a> nominated by the <a href="/wiki/1835_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1835 Democratic National Convention">1835 Democratic National Convention</a> at <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore, Maryland</a>. On the issue of slavery, Van Buren moved to obtain the support of southerners by assuring them that he opposed abolitionism and supported the maintaining of slavery in states where it had already existed.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> Regarding the national bank, Van Buren made clear that he opposed rechartering a <a href="/wiki/Second_Bank_of_the_United_States" title="Second Bank of the United States">national bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> To demonstrate <a href="/wiki/Good_faith" title="Good faith">consistency</a> regarding his opinions on slavery, Van Buren cast the tie-breaking Senate vote in favor of <a href="/wiki/Enrolled_bill" title="Enrolled bill">engrossing</a> a bill to subject <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">abolitionist</a> mail to state laws, thus ensuring that its circulation would be prohibited in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Martin Van Buren's competitors <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1836" title="United States presidential election, 1836">in the 1836 election</a> were the <a href="/wiki/Whig_Party_(United_States)" title="Whig Party (United States)">Whigs</a>; they ran several regional candidates in hopes of sending the election to the House of Representatives, where each state delegation would have one vote and the Whigs would stand a better chance of winning.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> hoped to receive the support of the Western voters, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Webster" title="Daniel Webster">Daniel Webster</a> had strength in New England, and <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Lawson_White" title="Hugh Lawson White">Hugh Lawson White</a> and <a href="/wiki/Willie_Person_Mangum" title="Willie Person Mangum">Willie Person Mangum</a> had support in the South. Van Buren won the election easily, with 170 electoral votes to 73 for Harrison, 26 for White, 14 for Webster and 11 for Mangum.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Twentieth-century etymologist <a href="/wiki/Allen_Walker_Read" title="Allen Walker Read">Allen Walker Read</a> published research asserting the wide usage of the phrase "O.K." (<a href="/wiki/Okay" title="Okay" class="mw-redirect">okay</a>) -- "<b>O</b>ld <b>K</b>inderhook"—started during the presidential campaign and subsequent presidency of Martin Van Buren.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_1837.E2.80.931841">Presidency 1837–1841</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Presidency 1837–1841">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies">Policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 250px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -80px; left: -60px; width: 350px"><a href="/wiki/File:VAN_BUREN,_Martin-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Van Buren as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/350px-VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="350" height="406" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/525px-VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/700px-VAN_BUREN%2C_Martin-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7912" data-file-height="9170" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VAN_BUREN,_Martin-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:VAN BUREN, Martin-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:VAN BUREN, Martin-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Van Buren as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Postage stamp with the image of a bust of a balding man in profile and facing right" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg/220px-Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg" width="220" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg/330px-Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg/440px-Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="535" data-file-height="620" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren3_1903_Issue-8c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Martin Van Buren</center>
<center><span style="font-size:9pt"><a href="/wiki/U.S._Presidents_on_U.S._postage_stamps" title="U.S. Presidents on U.S. postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">Issue of 1938</a></span></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A golden coin with a portrait of a balding man wearing a cravat and facing forward" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/220px-Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/330px-Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg/440px-Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_%241_Coin_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1098" data-file-height="1098" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Van_Buren_Presidential_$1_Coin_obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Presidential_$1_Coin_Program" title="Presidential $1 Coin Program">2008 Presidential Dollar of Martin Van Buren</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Martin Van Buren announced his intention "to follow in the footsteps of his illustrious predecessor", and retained all but one of Jackson's cabinet. Van Buren had few economic tools to deal with the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1837" title="Panic of 1837">Panic of 1837</a>. The Panic was followed by a five-year <a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">depression</a> in which banks failed and <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a> reached record highs.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> Some modern economists have argued that the Panic was caused by the Jackson administration's bank policies, with the power to create money being distributed into decentralized banks (most of which would then continue to cause a massive inflationary bubble).<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Van Buren advocated lower tariffs and free trade, and by doing so maintained support of the South for the Democratic Party. He succeeded in setting up a system of bonds for the national debt. His party was so split that his 1837 proposal for an "Independent Treasury" system did not pass until 1840. It gave the Treasury control of all federal funds and had a legal tender clause that required, by 1843, all payments to be made in <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">specie</a>, but further inflamed public opinion on both sides.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In a bold step, Van Buren reversed Andrew Jackson's policies and sought peace at home, as well as abroad. Instead of settling a financial dispute between American citizens and the Mexican government by force, Van Buren wanted to seek a diplomatic solution. In August 1837, Van Buren denied <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>' formal request to join the United States, again prioritizing sectional harmony over territorial expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> This action particularly angered pro-slavery leaders, who thought he did not want to admit Texas as a slave state per the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a>.</p>
<p>In the case of the ship <i><a href="/wiki/La_Amistad" title="La Amistad">Amistad</a></i>, Van Buren sided with the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Spain" title="Government of Spain">Spanish government</a> to return the kidnapped slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> Regarding Indian removal, Van Buren oversaw the movement of <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_(Creek)" title="Muscogee (Creek)" class="mw-redirect">Creek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> tribes from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_territory" title="Oklahoma territory" class="mw-redirect">Oklahoma territory</a>, executing the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act_of_1830" title="Indian Removal Act of 1830" class="mw-redirect">orders passed under Jackson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> To help secure <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, Van Buren also continued the <a href="/wiki/Second_Seminole_War" title="Second Seminole War">Second Seminole War</a>, which had begun during Jackson's presidency. Fighting was not resolved until 1842, after Van Buren had left office.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1839, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith, Jr.</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a>, visited Van Buren to plead for the U.S. to help roughly 20,000 Mormon settlers of <a href="/wiki/Independence,_Missouri" title="Independence, Missouri">Independence, Missouri</a> (who were forced from the state during the <a href="/wiki/Mormon_War_(1838)" title="Mormon War (1838)" class="mw-redirect">1838 Mormon War</a>) there. The Governor of Missouri, <a href="/wiki/Lilburn_Boggs" title="Lilburn Boggs">Lilburn Boggs</a>, had issued <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Executive_Order_44" title="Missouri Executive Order 44">an executive order</a> on October 27, 1838, known as the "Extermination Order". It authorized troops to use force against Mormons to "exterminate or drive [them] from the state".<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1839, after moving to Illinois, Smith and his party appealed to members of Congress and to President Van Buren to intercede for the Mormons. According to Smith's grandnephew, Van Buren said to Smith, "Your cause is just, but I can do nothing for you; if I take up for you I shall lose the vote of Missouri".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While in the state Senate Van Buren voted for a resolution instructing New York's members of Congress to vote against the admission of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> as a slave state. In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1848" title="United States presidential election, 1848">1848</a> he would be the nominated for president by the <a href="/wiki/Free_Soil_Party" title="Free Soil Party">Free Soil Party</a> (an anti-slavery political party).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite these antislavery views, during his term of office there was no ambiguity about his position on the abolition of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Van Buren actually considered slavery immoral, but sanctioned by the Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> He was against its abolition both in D.C. and in the United States altogether, and said so in his Inaugural Address in 1837: "I believed it a solemn duty fully to make known my sentiments in regard to it [slavery], and now, when every motive for misrepresentation has passed away, I trust that they will be candidly weighed and understood.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>"I must go into the Presidential chair the inflexible and uncompromising opponent of every attempt on the part of Congress to abolish slavery in the District of Columbia against the wishes of the slaveholding States, and also with a determination equally decided to resist the slightest interference with it in the States where it exists."<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Slavery would be abolished in the District of Columbia on April 18, 1862.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_Cabinet">Administration and Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Van_Buren&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Administration and Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Engraved full-length portrait of a balding man standing next to a table with his left arm resting on a book and in the background a stone balustrade beyond which are trees and a building with columned portico" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg/220px-MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg/330px-MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg/440px-MVan_Buren-portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4072" data-file-height="5904" /></a>

Portrait of Martin Van Buren</div>
</div>
